	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Atlantic puffin</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Puffin&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Puffin">Atlantic Puffin</a>)</span></div>

<p>The <b>Atlantic puffin</b> (<i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Fratercula arctica</i></i>), also known as the <b>common puffin</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">auk</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a>. It is the only <a href="/wiki/Puffin" title="Puffin">puffin</a> native to the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>; two related species, the <a href="/wiki/Tufted_puffin" title="Tufted puffin">tufted puffin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Horned_puffin" title="Horned puffin">horned puffin</a>, are found in the northeastern <a href="/wiki/Pacific" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific">Pacific</a>. The Atlantic puffin breeds in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, and as far south as <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> in the west and parts of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> in the east. The Atlantic Puffin is most commonly found on the <a href="/wiki/Westman_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Westman Islands">Westman Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>.  Although it has a large population and a wide range, the species has declined rapidly, at least in parts of its range, resulting in it being rated as <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">vulnerable</a> by the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>. On land, it has the typical upright stance of an auk. At sea, it swims on the surface and feeds mainly on small <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, which it catches by diving under water, using its wings for propulsion.
</p><p>This puffin has a black crown and back, pale grey cheek patches and white underparts. Its broad, boldly marked red and black beak and orange legs contrast with its plumage. It <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">moults</a> while at sea in the winter and some of the bright-coloured facial characteristics are lost, with color returning again during the spring. The external appearance of the adult male and female are identical, though the male is usually slightly larger. The juvenile has similar plumage, but its cheek patches are dark grey. The juvenile does not have brightly coloured head ornamentation, its bill is narrower and is dark-grey with a yellowish-brown tip, and its legs and feet are also dark. Puffins from northern populations are typically larger than in the south and  these populations are generally considered a different subspecies.
</p><p>Spending the autumn and winter in the open ocean of the cold northern seas, the Atlantic puffin returns to coastal areas at the start of the breeding season in late spring. It nests in clifftop <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">colonies</a>, digging a burrow in which a single white egg is laid. The chick mostly feeds on whole fish and grows rapidly. After about 6 weeks, it is fully <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledged</a> and makes its way at night to the sea. It swims away from the shore and does not return to land for several years.
</p><p>Colonies are mostly on islands with no terrestrial predators, but adult birds and newly fledged chicks are at risk of attacks from the air by <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">skuas</a>. Sometimes, a bird such as an <a href="/wiki/Arctic_skua" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic skua">Arctic skua</a> will <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">harass</a> a puffin arriving with a beakful of fish, causing it to drop its catch. The striking appearance, large colourful bill, waddling gait, and behaviour of this bird have given rise to nicknames such as "clown of the sea" and "sea parrot".<sup id="cite_ref-pitt1948_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pitt1948-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ap2013_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap2013-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It is the official bird symbol for the Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a>.
</p><p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> changed its status from "<a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a>" to "<a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">vulnerable</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  In 2018, <a href="/wiki/BirdLife_International" title="BirdLife International">BirdLife International</a> reported that the Atlantic puffin was threatened with extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_etymology">Taxonomy and etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; background-color: white; vertical-align: middle; width: 248px; margin:0.2em 0 0.2em 1em; float:right; clear:right;">



<tbody><tr>
<td style="border: 1px solid #dedede; padding: 3px">

<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;">Cladogram of the family <a href="/wiki/Alcidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcidae">Alcidae</a><sup id="cite_ref-smith2011_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith2011-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Atlantic puffin is a species of seabird in the order <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">Charadriiformes</a>. It is in the auk <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcidae">Alcidae</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Guillemot" title="Guillemot">guillemots</a>, typical auks, <a href="/wiki/Synthliboramphus" title="Synthliboramphus">murrelets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auklet" class="mw-redirect" title="Auklet">auklets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puffin" title="Puffin">puffins</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Razorbill" title="Razorbill">razorbill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros_auklet" title="Rhinoceros auklet">rhinoceros auklet</a> (<i>Cerorhinca monocerata</i>) and the puffins are closely related, together composing the <a href="/wiki/Tribe_(biology)" title="Tribe (biology)">tribe</a> Fraterculini.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The Atlantic puffin is the only species in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Fratercula" class="mw-redirect" title="Fratercula">Fratercula</a></i> to occur in the Atlantic Ocean. Two other species are known from the northeast Pacific, the <a href="/wiki/Tufted_puffin" title="Tufted puffin">tufted puffin</a> (<i>Fratercula cirrhata</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Horned_puffin" title="Horned puffin">horned puffin</a> (<i>Fratercula corniculata</i>), the latter being the closest relative of the Atlantic puffin.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Scientific_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific name">generic name</a> <i>Fratercula</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> <i>fratercula</i>, <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friar</a>, a reference to the black and white plumage which resembles <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monastic</a> robes.<sup id="cite_ref-DSBN_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSBN-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The specific name <i>arctica</i> refers to the <a href="/wiki/Boreal_ecosystem" title="Boreal ecosystem">northerly distribution</a> of the bird, being derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> ἄρκτος ("arktos"), the <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bear</a>, referring to the northerly <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Major" title="Ursa Major">Great Bear</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The vernacular name "puffin" – puffed in the sense of swollen – was originally applied to the fatty, salted meat of young birds of the unrelated species <a href="/wiki/Manx_shearwater" title="Manx shearwater">Manx shearwater</a> (<i>Puffinus puffinus</i>), which in 1652 was known as the "Manks puffin".<sup id="cite_ref-ODBBN_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODBBN-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is an Anglo-Norman word (<a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <i>pophyn</i> or <i>poffin</i>) used for the cured carcasses.<sup id="cite_ref-OED_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The Atlantic puffin acquired the name at a much later stage, possibly because of its similar nesting habits,<sup id="cite_ref-leehaney1996_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leehaney1996-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and it was formally applied to <i>Fratercula arctica</i> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pennant" title="Thomas Pennant">Pennant</a> in 1768.<sup id="cite_ref-ODBBN_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODBBN-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> While the species is also known as the common puffin, "Atlantic puffin" is the English name recommended by the <a href="/wiki/International_Ornithological_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="International Ornithological Congress">International Ornithological Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The three subspecies generally recognized are:<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>F. a. arctica</i></li>
<li><i>F. a.  grabae</i></li>
<li><i>F. a.  naumanni</i></li></ul>
<p>The only <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> difference between the three is their size. Body length, wing length, and size of beak all increase at higher latitudes. For example, a puffin from northern Iceland (subspecies <i>F. a. naumanii</i>) weighs about 650 grams (1.4&#160;lb) and has a wing length of 186&#160;mm (7.3&#160;in), while one from the <a href="/wiki/Faroes" class="mw-redirect" title="Faroes">Faroes</a> (subspecies <i>F. a. grabae</i>) weighs 400&#160;g (0.9&#160;lb) and has a wing length of 158&#160;mm (6.2&#160;in). Individuals from southern Iceland (subspecies <i>F. a. arctica</i>) are intermediate between the other two in size.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a> has argued that the differences in size are <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinal</a> and are typical of variations found in peripheral population and that no subspecies should be recognised.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Head of a puffin showing its colourful beak" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg/220px-Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg/330px-Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg/440px-Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3900" data-file-height="2786" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Puffin_Fratercula_arctica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult puffins have boldly coloured beaks in the breeding season.</div></div></div>
<p>The Atlantic puffin is sturdily built with a thick-set neck and short wings and tail. It is 28 to 30&#160;cm (11 to 12&#160;in) in length from the tip of its stout <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">bill</a> to its blunt-ended tail. Its wingspan is 47 to 63&#160;cm (19 to 25&#160;in) and on land it stands about 20&#160;cm (8&#160;in) high. The male is generally slightly larger than the female, but they are coloured alike. The forehead, crown, and nape are glossy black, as are the back, wings, and tail. A broad, black collar extends around the neck and throat. On each side of the head is a large, lozenge-shaped area of very pale grey. These face patches taper to a point and nearly meet at the back of the neck. The shape of the head creates a crease extending from the eye to the hindmost point of each patch, giving the appearance of a grey streak. The eye looks almost triangular in shape because of a small, peaked area of horny blue-grey skin above it and a rectangular patch below. The <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">irises</a> are brown or very dark blue and each has red orbital ring. The under parts of the bird, the breast, belly and under tail coverts, are white. By the end of the breeding season, the black plumage may have lost its shine or even taken on a slightly brown tinge. The legs are short and set well back on the body, giving the bird its upright stance when on land. Both legs and large <a href="/wiki/Webbed_feet" class="mw-redirect" title="Webbed feet">webbed feet</a> are bright orange, contrasting with the sharp, black claws.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19–23</span></sup>
</p><p>The beak is very distinctive. From the side, the beak is broad and triangular, but viewed from above, it is narrow. The half near the tip is orange-red and the half near to the head is <a href="/wiki/Slate_gray" title="Slate gray">slate grey</a>. A yellow, chevron-shaped ridge separates the two parts, with a yellow, fleshy strip at the base of the bill. At the joint of the two mandibles  is a yellow, wrinkled rosette. The exact proportions of the beak vary with the age of the bird. In an immature individual, the beak has reached its full length, but it is not as broad as that of an adult. With time the bill deepens, the upper edge curves, and a kink develops at its base. As the bird ages, one or more grooves may form on the red portion.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19–23</span></sup> The bird has a powerful bite.<sup id="cite_ref-geolocator_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geolocator-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Beak before and after moulting" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg/220px-NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="95" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg/330px-NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg/440px-NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="763" data-file-height="328" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NIE_1905_Puffin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Appearance of beak and eyes during the breeding season (left) and after the moult (right; lettered items have been shed)</div></div></div>
<p>The characteristic bright orange bill plates and other facial characteristics develop in the spring. At the close of the breeding season, these special coatings and appendages are shed in a partial moult.<sup id="cite_ref-atlanticpuffinref15_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlanticpuffinref15-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This makes the beak appear less broad, the tip less bright, and the base darker grey. The eye ornaments are shed and the eyes appear round. At the same time, the feathers of the head and neck are replaced and the face becomes darker.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> This winter plumage is seldom seen by humans because when they have left their chicks, the birds head out to sea and do not return to land until the next breeding season. The juvenile bird is similar to the adult in plumage, but altogether duller with a much darker grey face and yellowish-brown beak tip and legs. After fledging, it makes its way to the water and heads out to sea and does not return to land for several years. In the interim, each year, it will have a broader bill, paler face patches, and brighter legs and beak.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19–23</span></sup>
</p><p>The Atlantic puffin has a direct flight, typically 10&#160;m (33&#160;ft) above the sea surface and higher over the water than most other auks.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> It mostly moves by paddling along efficiently with its webbed feet and seldom takes to the air.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>43</span></sup> It is typically silent at sea, except for the soft purring sounds it sometimes makes in flight. At the <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">breeding colony</a>, it is quiet above ground, but in its burrow makes a growling sound somewhat resembling a <a href="/wiki/Chainsaw" title="Chainsaw">chainsaw</a> being revved up.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Atlantic puffin is a bird of the colder waters of the North Atlantic Ocean. It breeds on the coasts of northwest Europe, the Arctic fringes, and eastern North America. More than 90% of the global population is found in Europe (4,770,000–5,780,000 pairs, equaling 9,550,000–11,600,000 adults)<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> alone are home to 60% of the world's Atlantic puffins. The largest colony in the western Atlantic (estimated at more than 260,000 pairs) can be found at the <a href="/wiki/Witless_Bay_Ecological_Reserve" title="Witless Bay Ecological Reserve">Witless Bay Ecological Reserve</a>, south of <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="St. John&#39;s, Newfoundland and Labrador">St. John's, Newfoundland and Labrador</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Other major breeding locations include the north and west coasts of Norway, the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> Islands, the west coast of <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>. Smaller-sized colonies are also found elsewhere in the British Isles, the <a href="/wiki/Murmansk" title="Murmansk">Murmansk</a> area of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novaya_Zemlya" title="Novaya Zemlya">Novaya Zemlya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spitzbergen" class="mw-redirect" title="Spitzbergen">Spitzbergen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>. Islands seem particularly attractive to the birds for breeding as compared to mainland sites.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24–29</span></sup>
</p><p>While at sea, the bird ranges widely across the North Atlantic Ocean, including the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, and may enter the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>. In the summer, its southern limit stretches from northern <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> to Maine and in the winter, the bird may range as far south as the Mediterranean Sea and <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>. These oceanic waters have such a vast extent of 15 to 30 million km<sup>2</sup> (6 to 12 million mi<sup>2</sup>) that each bird has more than 1&#160;km<sup>2</sup> at its disposal and they are seldom seen out at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>30</span></sup> In Maine, <a href="/wiki/Light_level_geolocator" title="Light level geolocator">light level geolocators</a> have been attached to the legs of puffins, which store information on their whereabouts. The birds need to be recaptured to access the information, a difficult task. One bird was found to have covered 4,800 miles (7,700&#160;km) of ocean in eight months, traveling northwards to the northern <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a> then southeastward to the mid-Atlantic before returning to land.<sup id="cite_ref-geolocator_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geolocator-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a long-living bird with a small clutch size such as the Atlantic puffin, the survival rate of adults is an important factor influencing the success of the species. Only 5% of the <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> puffins that failed to reappear at the colony did so during the breeding season. The rest were lost some time between departing from land in the summer and reappearing the following spring. The birds spend the winter widely spread out in the open ocean, though a tendency exists for individuals from different colonies to overwinter in different areas. Little is known of their behaviour and diet at sea, but no correlation was found between environmental factors, such as temperature variations, and their mortality rate. A combination of the availability of food in winter and summer probably influences the survival of the birds, since individuals starting the winter in poor condition are less likely to survive than those in good condition.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like many seabirds, the Atlantic puffin spends most of the year far from land in the open ocean and only visits coastal areas to breed. It is a sociable bird and it usually breeds in large colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Atlas_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="At_sea">At sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: At sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 352px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width: 350px; height: 150px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -80px; left: -40px; width: 500px"><div class="noresize"><a href="/wiki/File:NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_(Fratercula_arctica).jpg" class="image"><img alt="A running takeoff at Bear Island (Norway)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_%28Fratercula_arctica%29.jpg/500px-NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_%28Fratercula_arctica%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="500" height="333" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_%28Fratercula_arctica%29.jpg/750px-NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_%28Fratercula_arctica%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_%28Fratercula_arctica%29.jpg/1000px-NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_%28Fratercula_arctica%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3375" data-file-height="2250" /></a></div></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NOR-2016-Bear_Island-Atlantic_puffin_(Fratercula_arctica).jpg" title="File:NOR-2016-Bear Island-Atlantic puffin (Fratercula arctica).jpg"> </a></div>A running takeoff at <a href="/wiki/Bear_Island_(Norway)" title="Bear Island (Norway)">Bear Island (Norway)</a>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="A puffin floating on the sea" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_%282%29.jpg/220px-Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_%282%29.jpg/330px-Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_%282%29.jpg/440px-Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3432" data-file-height="3056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Macareux_en_p%C3%AAche_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bobbing about on the waves off the coast of northern Norway</div></div></div>
<p>Atlantic puffins lead solitary existences when out at sea, and this part of their lives has been little studied, as the task of finding even one bird on the vast ocean is formidable. When at sea, the Atlantic puffin bobs about like a <a href="/wiki/Cork_(material)" title="Cork (material)">cork</a>, propelling itself through the water with powerful thrusts of its feet and keeping itself turned into the wind, even when resting and apparently asleep. It spends much time each day preening to keep its plumage in order and spreads oil from the <a href="/wiki/Uropygial_gland" title="Uropygial gland">preen gland</a>. Its downy under-plumage remains dry and provides thermal insulation. In common with other seabirds, its upper surface is black and underside white. This provides <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a>, with aerial <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a> unable to observe the bird against the dark, watery background and underwater attackers failing to notice it as it blends in with the bright sky above the waves.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>30–43</span></sup>
</p><p>When it takes off, the Atlantic puffin patters across the surface of the water while vigorously flapping its wings, before launching itself into the air.<sup id="cite_ref-atlanticpuffinref15_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlanticpuffinref15-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>30–43</span></sup> The size of the wing has adapted to its dual use, both above and below the water, and its surface area is small relative to the bird's weight. To maintain flight, the wings need to beat very rapidly at a rate of several times each second.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The bird's flight is direct and low over the surface of the water and it can travel at 80&#160;km (50&#160;mi) per hour. Landing is awkward; it either crashes into a wave crest, or in calmer water, does a belly flop. While at sea, the Atlantic puffin has its annual <a href="/wiki/Moulting#Birds" title="Moulting">moult</a>. Land birds mostly lose their <a href="/wiki/Primary_feather#Primaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary feather">primaries</a> one pair at a time to enable them still to be able to fly, but the puffin sheds all its primaries at one time and dispenses with flight entirely for a month or two. The moult usually takes place between January and March, but young birds may lose their feathers a little later in the year.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>30–43</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_feeding">Food and feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Food and feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Atlantic puffin diet consists almost entirely of fish, though examination of its stomach contents shows that it occasionally eats <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mollusc" class="mw-redirect" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polychaete" title="Polychaete">polychaete worms</a>, especially in more coastal waters.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> When fishing, it swims under water using its semiextended wings as <a href="/wiki/Paddles" class="mw-redirect" title="Paddles">paddles</a> to "fly" through the water and its feet as a rudder. It swims fast and can reach considerable depths and stay submerged for up to a minute. It can eat shallow-bodied fish as long as 18&#160;cm (7&#160;in), but its prey is commonly smaller fish, around 7&#160;cm (3&#160;in) long. An adult bird needs to eat an estimated 40 of these per day—<a href="/wiki/Sand_eel" title="Sand eel">sand eels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sprat" title="Sprat">sprats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Capelin" title="Capelin">capelin</a> being the most often consumed. It fishes by sight and can swallow small fish while submerged, but larger specimens are brought to the surface. It can catch several small fish in one dive, holding the first ones in place in its beak with its muscular, grooved tongue while it catches others. The two mandibles are hinged in such a way that they can be held parallel to hold a row of fish in place and these are also retained by inward-facing serrations on the edges of the beak. It copes with the excess salt that it swallows partly through its <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> and partly by <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretion</a> through specialised <a href="/wiki/Salt_gland" title="Salt gland">salt glands</a> in its nostrils.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>30–43</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_land">On land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: On land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_1" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:640px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_2&quot; <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Birds on rocks" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="632" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Puffin_gang.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relaxation in the colony</div></div></div>
<p>In the spring, mature birds return to land, usually to the colony where they were hatched. Birds that were removed as chicks and released elsewhere were found to show fidelity to their point of liberation.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> They congregate for a few days on the sea in small groups offshore before returning to the cliff-top nesting sites. Each large puffin colony is divided into subcolonies by physical boundaries such as stands of <a href="/wiki/Bracken" title="Bracken">bracken</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gorse" class="mw-redirect" title="Gorse">gorse</a>. Early arrivals take control of the best locations, the most desirable nesting sites being the densely packed burrows on grassy slopes just above the cliff edge where take-off is most easily accomplished. The birds are usually <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamous</a>, but this is the result of their fidelity to their nesting sites rather than to their mates, and they often return to the same burrows year after year. Later arrivals at the colony may find that all the best nesting sites have already been taken, so are pushed towards the periphery, where they are in greater danger of predation. Younger birds may come ashore a month or more after the mature birds and find no remaining nesting sites. They do not breed until the following year, although  if the ground cover surrounding the colony is cut back before these subadults arrive, the number of successfully nesting pairs may be increased.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>44–65</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Puffins,_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Establishing dominance" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Atlantic_Puffins%2C_Scotland.jpg/220px-Atlantic_Puffins%2C_Scotland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Atlantic_Puffins%2C_Scotland.jpg/330px-Atlantic_Puffins%2C_Scotland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Atlantic_Puffins%2C_Scotland.jpg/440px-Atlantic_Puffins%2C_Scotland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3120" data-file-height="1893" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Puffins,_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Establishing dominance</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island,_Wales_-two-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Pair outside burrow" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island%2C_Wales_-two-8.jpg/220px-Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island%2C_Wales_-two-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island%2C_Wales_-two-8.jpg/330px-Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island%2C_Wales_-two-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island%2C_Wales_-two-8.jpg/440px-Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island%2C_Wales_-two-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1545" data-file-height="1030" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fratercula_arctica_-Skomer_Island,_Wales_-two-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pair outside burrow on <a href="/wiki/Skomer_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Skomer Island">Skomer Island</a>, Wales</div></div></div>
<p>Atlantic puffins are cautious when approaching the colony, and no bird likes to land in a location where other puffins are not already present. They make several circuits of the colony before alighting. On the ground, they spend much time preening, spreading oil from their preen gland and setting each feather in its correct position with beak or claw. They also spend time standing by their burrow entrances and interacting with passing birds. <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)" title="Dominance (ethology)">Dominance</a> is shown by an upright stance, with fluffed chest feathers and cocked tail, an exaggerated slow walk, head jerking, and gaping. Submissive birds lower their heads and hold their bodies horizontal and scurry past dominant individuals. Birds normally signal their intention to take off by briefly lowering their bodies before running down the slope to gain momentum. If a bird is startled and takes off unexpectedly, a panic can spread through the colony with all the birds launching themselves into the air and wheeling around in a great circle. The colony is at its most active in the evening, with birds standing outside their burrows, resting on the turf or strolling around. Then, the slopes empty for the night as the birds fly out to sea to <a href="/wiki/Bird#Resting_and_roosting" title="Bird">roost</a>, often choosing to do so at fishing grounds ready for early-morning provisioning.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>44–65</span></sup>
</p><p>The puffins are energetic burrow-engineers and -repairers and the grassy slopes may be undermined by a network of tunnels. This causes the turf to dry out in summer, vegetation to die, and dry soil be whirled away by the wind. Burrows sometimes collapse, and humans may cause this to happen by walking incautiously across nesting slopes. A colony on <a href="/wiki/Grassholm" title="Grassholm">Grassholm</a> was lost through erosion when so little soil was left that burrows could not be made.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>48</span></sup> New colonies are very unlikely to start up spontaneously because this gregarious bird only nests where others are already present. Nevertheless, the <a href="/wiki/Audubon_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Audubon Society">Audubon Society</a> had success on <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Egg_Rock_Island" title="Eastern Egg Rock Island">Eastern Egg Rock Island</a> in Maine, where, after a gap of 90 years, puffins were reintroduced and started breeding again. By 2011, over 120 pairs nested on the small islet.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> On the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_May" title="Isle of May">Isle of May</a> on the other side of the Atlantic, only five pairs of puffins were breeding in 1958, while 20 years later, 10,000 pairs were present.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>47</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG/220px-Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG/330px-Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG/440px-Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%ADmsey_Iceland.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical Atlantic puffin breeding habitat in Iceland</div></div></div>
<p>Having spent the winter alone on the ocean,  whether the Atlantic puffin meets its previous partner offshore or whether they encounter each other when they return to their nest of the previous year is unclear. On land, they soon set about improving and clearing out the burrow. Often, one stands outside the entrance while the other excavates, kicking out quantities of soil and grit that showers the partner standing outside. Some birds collect stems and fragments of dry grasses as nesting materials, but others do not bother. Sometimes, a beakful of materials is taken underground, only to be brought out again and discarded. Apart from nest-building, the other way in which the birds restore their bond is by <a href="/wiki/Billing_(birds)#Billing" class="mw-redirect" title="Billing (birds)">billing</a>. This is a practice in which the pair approaches each other, each wagging their heads from side to side, and then rattling their beaks together. This seems to be an important element of their <a href="/wiki/Courtship" title="Courtship">courtship</a> behaviour because it happens repeatedly, and the birds continue to bill, to a lesser extent, throughout the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>72</span></sup>
</p><p>The Atlantic puffin is sexually mature at the age of 4–5 years. The birds are colonial nesters, excavating burrows on grassy clifftops or reusing existing holes, and on occasion may nest in crevices and among rocks and scree. It is in competition with other birds and animals for burrows. It can excavate its own hole or move into a pre-existing system dug by a <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbit</a>, and has been known to peck and drive off the original occupant. Manx shearwaters also nest underground and often live in their own burrows alongside puffins, and their burrowing activities may break through into the puffin's living quarters, resulting in the loss of the egg.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>107</span></sup> They are monogamous (they mate for life) and give biparental care to their young. The male spends more time guarding and maintaining the nest, while the female is more involved in incubation and feeding the chick.<sup id="cite_ref-Creelman_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creelman-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Egg-laying starts in April in more southerly colonies, but seldom occurs before June in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>. The female lays a single white <a href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (biology)">egg</a> each year, but if this is lost early in the breeding season, another might be produced.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>78–81</span></sup> Synchronous laying of eggs is found in Atlantic puffins in adjacent burrows.<sup id="cite_ref-ehrlichetal1988_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ehrlichetal1988-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The egg is large compared to the size of the bird, averaging 61&#160;mm (2.4&#160;in) long by 42&#160;mm (1.7&#160;in) wide and weighing about 62&#160;g (2.2&#160;oz). The white shell is usually devoid of markings, but soon becomes soiled with mud. The <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">incubation</a> responsibilities are shared by both parents. They each have two feather-free <a href="/wiki/Brood_patch" title="Brood patch">brood patches</a> on their under sides, where an enhanced blood supply provides heat for the egg. The parent on incubation duty in the dark nest chamber spends much of its time asleep with its head tucked under its wing, occasionally emerging from the tunnel to flap dust out of its feathers or take a short flight down to the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>78–81</span></sup>
</p><p>Total incubation time is around 39–45 days. From above ground level, the first evidence that hatching has taken place is the arrival of an adult with a beak-load of fish. For the first few days, the chick may be fed with these beak-to-beak, but later the fish are simply dropped on the floor of the nest beside the chick, which swallows them whole. The chick is covered in fluffy black down and its eyes are open and it can stand as soon as it is hatched. Initially weighing about 42&#160;g (1.5&#160;oz), it grows at the rate of 10&#160;g (0.35&#160;oz) per day. Initially, one or the other parent broods it, but as its appetite increases, it is left alone for longer periods. Observations of a nest chamber have been made from an underground hide with peephole. The chick sleeps much of the time between its parents' visits and also involves itself in bouts of exercise. It rearranges its nesting material, picks up and drops small stones, flaps its immature wings, pulls at protruding root ends, and pushes and strains against the unyielding wall of the burrow. It makes its way towards the entrance or along a side tunnel to defecate. The growing chick seems to anticipate the arrival of an adult, advancing along the burrow just before it arrives, but not emerging into the open air. It retreats to the nest chamber as the adult bird brings in its load of fish.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>82–95</span></sup>
</p>

			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul></div></div></div>
<p>Hunting areas are often located 100&#160;km (60&#160;mi) or more, offshore from the nest sites, although when feeding their young, the birds venture out only half that distance.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Adults bringing fish to their chicks tend to arrive in groups. This is thought to benefit the bird by reducing <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_skua" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic skua">Arctic skua</a>, which harasses puffins until they drop their fish loads. Predation by the <a href="/wiki/Great_skua" title="Great skua">great skua</a> (<i>Catharacta skua</i>) is also reduced by several birds arriving simultaneously.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Islands">Shetland Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raitt%27s_sand_eel" title="Raitt&#39;s sand eel">sand eels</a> (<i>Ammodytes marinus</i>) normally form at least 90% of the food fed to chicks. In years where the availability of sand eels was low, breeding success rates fell, with many chicks starving to death.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_herring" title="Atlantic herring">herring</a> (<i>Clupea harengus</i>) is the mainstay of the diet. When herring numbers dwindled, so did puffin numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In Labrador, the puffins seemed more flexible and when the staple <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a> capelin (<i>Mallotus villosus</i>) declined in availability, they were able to adapt and feed the chicks on other prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The chicks take from 34 to 50 days to <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a>, the period depending on the abundance of their food supply. In years of fish shortage, the whole colony may experience a longer <a href="/wiki/Fledgling_(birds)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fledgling (birds)">fledgling</a> period, but the normal range is 38 to 44 days, by which time chicks will have reached about 75% of their mature body weight. The chick may come to the burrow entrance to defecate, but does not usually emerge into the open<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>85–99</span></sup> and seems to have an aversion to light until it is nearly fully fledged.<sup id="cite_ref-Couzens_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Couzens-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Although the supply of fish by the adults reduces over the last few days spent in the nest, the chick is not abandoned as happens in the Manx shearwater. On occasions, an adult has been observed provisioning a nest even after the chick has departed. During the last few days underground, the chick sheds its down and the juvenile plumage is revealed. Its relatively small beak and its legs and feet are a dark colour and it lacks the white facial patches of the adult. The chick finally leaves its nest at night, when the risk of predation is at its lowest. When the moment arrives, it emerges from the burrow, usually for the first time, and walks, runs, and flaps its way to the sea. It cannot fly properly yet, so descending a cliff is perilous; when it reaches the water, it paddles out to sea, and may be 3&#160;km (2&#160;mi) away from the shore by daybreak. It does not congregate with others of its kind and does not return to land for 2–3 years.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>85–99</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators_and_parasites">Predators and parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Predators and parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Atlantic puffins are probably safer when out at sea. Here, the dangers are more often from below the water rather than above and puffins can sometimes be seen putting their heads under water to peer around for predators. <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">Seals</a> have been known to kill puffins and large fish may also do so. Most puffin colonies are on small islands, and this is no coincidence, as it avoids predation by ground-based <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> such as&#160;: <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoat" title="Stoat">stoats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>. When they come ashore, the birds are still at risk and the main threats come from the sky.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>102–103</span></sup>
</p><p>Aerial predators of the Atlantic puffin include the <a href="/wiki/Great_black-backed_gull" title="Great black-backed gull">great black-backed gull</a> (<i>Larus marinus</i>), the great skua (<i>Stercorarius skua</i>), and similar-sized species, which can catch a bird in flight, or attack one that is unable to escape fast enough on the ground. On detecting danger, puffins take off and fly down to the safety of the sea or retreat into their burrows, but if caught, they defend themselves vigorously with beak and sharp claws. When the puffins are wheeling round beside the cliffs,  a predator concentrating on a single bird becomes very difficult, while any individual isolated on the ground is at greater risk.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>51</span></sup> Smaller gull species like the <a href="/wiki/European_herring_gull" title="European herring gull">herring gull</a> (<i>L. argentatus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_black-backed_gull" title="Lesser black-backed gull">lesser black-backed gull</a> are hardly able to bring down a healthy adult puffin. They stride through the colony taking any eggs that have rolled towards burrow entrances or recently hatched chicks that have ventured too far towards the daylight. They also steal fish from puffins returning to feed their young.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Where it nests on the <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> in the far north, the Arctic skua (<i>Stercorarius parasiticus</i>) is a terrestrial predator, but at lower latitudes, it is a specialised kleptoparasite, concentrating on auks and other seabirds. It harasses puffins while they are airborne, forcing them to drop their catch, which it then snatches up.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both the guillemot <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">tick</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ixodes_uriae" title="Ixodes uriae">Ixodes uriae</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">flea</a> <i>Ornithopsylla laetitiae</i> (probably originally a rabbit flea) have been recorded from the nests of puffins. Other fleas found on the birds include <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratophyllus_borealis" title="Ceratophyllus borealis">Ceratophyllus borealis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratophyllus_gallinae" title="Ceratophyllus gallinae">Ceratophyllus gallinae</a></i>, <i>Ceratophyllus garei</i>, <i>Ceratophyllus vagabunda</i>, and the common rabbit flea <i><a href="/wiki/Spilopsyllus_cuniculi" title="Spilopsyllus cuniculi">Spilopsyllus cuniculi</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_and_conservation">Status and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Status and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth,_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="In flight" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg/220px-Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg/330px-Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg/440px-Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fratercula_arctica_-Firth_of_Forth,_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In flight over the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_May" title="Isle of May">Isle of May</a>, Scotland</div></div></div>
<p>The Atlantic puffin has an extensive range that covers over 1,620,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (630,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) and Europe, which holds more than 90% of the global population, is home to 4,770,000-5,780,000 pairs (equaling 9,550,000-11,600,000 adults). In 2015, the International Union for Conservation of Nature upgraded its status from "least concern" to "vulnerable". This was caused by a review that revealed a rapid and ongoing population decline in its European range.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Trends elsewhere are unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Some of the causes of population decline may be increased predation by gulls and skuas, the introduction of rats, cats, dogs, and foxes onto some islands used for nesting, contamination by toxic residues, drowning in fishing nets, declining food supplies, and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> On the island of <a href="/wiki/Lundy" title="Lundy">Lundy</a>, the number of puffins decreased from 3,500 pairs in 1939 to 10 pairs in 2000. This was mainly due to the rats that had proliferated on the island and were eating eggs and young chicks. Following the elimination of the rats,  populations hopefully will recover,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> and in 2005, a juvenile was seen, believed to be the first chick raised on the island for 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, puffin numbers increased considerably in the late 20th century in the North Sea, including on the Isle of May and the <a href="/wiki/Farne_Islands" title="Farne Islands">Farne Islands</a>, where numbers increased by about 10% per year. In the 2013 breeding season, nearly 40,000 pairs were recorded on the Farne Islands, a slight increase on the 2008 census and on the previous year's poor season, when some of the burrows flooded.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> This number is dwarfed by the Icelandic colonies with five million pairs breeding, the Atlantic puffin being the most populous bird on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Vestmannaeyjar" title="Vestmannaeyjar">Westman Islands</a>, where about half Iceland's puffins breed, the birds were almost driven to extinction by overharvesting around 1900 and a 30-year ban on hunting was put in place. When stocks recovered, a different method of harvesting was used and now hunting is maintained at a sustainable level.<sup id="cite_ref-IcelandWeb_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IcelandWeb-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, a further hunting ban covering the whole of Iceland was called for in 2011, although the puffin's lack of recent breeding success was being blamed on a diminution in food supply rather than overharvesting.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Since 2000, a sharp population decline has been seen in Iceland, Norway, the  Faroe Islands, and Greenland.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> A similar trend has been seen in the United Kingdom, where an increase in 1969-2000 appears to have been reversed.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle" title="Fair Isle">Fair Isle</a> colony was estimated at 20,200 individuals in 1986, but it had been almost halved by 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Based on current trends, the European population will decline an estimated 50-79% between 2000 and 2065.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>SOS Puffin is a conservation project at the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Seabird_Centre" title="Scottish Seabird Centre">Scottish Seabird Centre</a> at <a href="/wiki/North_Berwick" title="North Berwick">North Berwick</a> to save the puffins on islands in the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a>. Puffin numbers on the island of <a href="/wiki/Craigleith" title="Craigleith">Craigleith</a>, once one of the largest colonies in Scotland with 28,000 pairs, have declined dramatically to just a few thousand due to the invasion of a large introduced plant, the <a href="/wiki/Lavatera_arborea" class="mw-redirect" title="Lavatera arborea">tree mallow</a> (<i>Lavatera arborea</i>). This has spread across the island in dense thickets and prevents the puffins from finding suitable sites for burrowing and breeding. The project has the support of over 700 volunteers and progress has been made in cutting back the plants, with puffins returning in greater numbers to breed.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Another conservation measure undertaken by the centre is to encourage motorists to check under their cars in late summer before driving off, as young puffins, disorientated by the street lights, may land in the town and take shelter underneath the vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Project_Puffin" title="Project Puffin">Project Puffin</a> is an effort initiated in 1973 by Dr. Stephen W. Kress of the <a href="/wiki/National_Audubon_Society" title="National Audubon Society">National Audubon Society</a> to restore Atlantic puffins to nesting islands in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Maine" title="Gulf of Maine">Gulf of Maine</a>. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Egg_Rock_Island" title="Eastern Egg Rock Island">Eastern Egg Rock Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/Muscongus_Bay" title="Muscongus Bay">Muscongus Bay</a>, about 6&#160;mi (9.7&#160;km) away from <a href="/wiki/Pemaquid_Point" class="mw-redirect" title="Pemaquid Point">Pemaquid Point</a>, had been occupied by nesting puffins until 1885, when the birds disappeared because of overhunting. Counting on the fact young puffins usually return to breed on the same island where they fledged, a team of biologists and volunteers translocated 10– to 14-day-old nestlings from <a href="/wiki/Great_Island_(Newfoundland_and_Labrador)" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Island (Newfoundland and Labrador)">Great Island</a> in Newfoundland to Eastern Egg Rock. The young were placed into artificial sod <a href="/wiki/Burrow" title="Burrow">burrows</a>, and fed with vitamin-fortified fish daily for about one month. Such yearly translocations took place until 1986, with 954 young puffins being moved in total. Each year before fledging, the young were individually tagged. The first adults returned to the island by 1977.  Puffin decoys had been installed on the island to fool the puffins into thinking they were part of an established colony. This did not catch on at first, but in 1981, four pairs nested on the island. In 2014, 148 nesting pairs were counted on the island. In addition to demonstrating the feasibility of re-establishing a seabird colony, the project showed the usefulness of using decoys, and eventually call recordings and mirrors, to facilitate such reestablishment.<sup id="cite_ref-History_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the Atlantic puffin spends its winters on the open ocean, it is susceptible to human actions and catastrophes such as oil spills. Oiled plumage has a reduced ability to insulate and makes the bird more vulnerable to temperature fluctuations and less buoyant in the water.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Many birds die, and others, while attempting to remove the oil by preening, ingest and inhale toxins. This leads to inflammation of the airways and gut and in the longer term, damage to liver and kidneys. This trauma can contribute to a loss of reproductive success and harm to developing embryos.<sup id="cite_ref-ehrlichetal1988_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ehrlichetal1988-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> An oil spill occurring in winter, when the puffins are far out at sea, may affect them less than inshore birds as the <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> slicks soon get broken up and dispersed by the churning of the waves. When oiled birds get washed up on beaches around Atlantic coasts, only about 1.5% of the dead auks are puffins, but many others may have died far from land and sunk.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> After the oil tanker <a href="/wiki/SS_Torrey_Canyon" title="SS Torrey Canyon"><i>Torrey Canyon</i></a> shipwreck and oil spill in 1967, few dead puffins were recovered, but the number of puffins breeding in France the following year was reduced to 16% of its previous level.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Atlantic puffin and other pelagic birds are excellent <a href="/wiki/Bioindicator" title="Bioindicator">bioindicators</a> of the environment, as they occupy a high <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">trophic level</a>. <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">Heavy metals</a> and other pollutants are concentrated through the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>, and as fish are the primary food source for Atlantic puffins, the potential is great for them to <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioaccumulate">bioaccumulate</a> heavy metals such as mercury and arsenic. Measurements can be made on eggs, feathers, or internal organs and beached bird surveys, accompanied by chemical analysis of feathers, can be effective indicators of <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">marine pollution</a> by <a href="/wiki/Lipophilicity" title="Lipophilicity">lipophilic</a> substances, as well as metals. In fact, these surveys can be used to provide evidence of the adverse effects of a particular pollutant, using <a href="/wiki/Community_Fingerprinting" class="mw-redirect" title="Community Fingerprinting">fingerprinting techniques</a> to provide evidence suitable for the prosecution of offenders.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> may well affect populations of seabirds in the northern Atlantic. The most important demographic may be an increase in the sea surface temperature, which may have benefits for some northerly Atlantic puffin colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Breeding success depends on ample supplies of food at the time of maximum demand, as the chick grows. In northern Norway, the main food item fed to the chick is the young herring. The success of the newly hatched fish larvae during the previous year was governed by the water temperature, which controlled plankton abundance, and this, in turn, influenced the growth and survival of the first-year herring. The breeding success of Atlantic puffin colonies has been found to correlate in this way with the water surface temperatures of the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Maine, on the other side of the Atlantic, shifting fish populations due to changes in sea temperature are being blamed for the lack of availability of the herring, which is the staple diet of the puffins in the area. Some adult birds have become emaciated and died. Others have been provisioning the nest with <a href="/wiki/American_butterfish" title="American butterfish">butterfish</a> (<i>Peprilus triacanthus</i>), but these are often too large and deep-bodied for the chick to swallow, causing it to die from starvation. Maine is on the southerly edge of the bird's breeding range, and with changing weather patterns, this may be set to contract northwards.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Puffin_watching_(8958978651).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Watching puffins" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Puffin_watching_%288958978651%29.jpg/220px-Puffin_watching_%288958978651%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Puffin_watching_%288958978651%29.jpg/330px-Puffin_watching_%288958978651%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Puffin_watching_%288958978651%29.jpg/440px-Puffin_watching_%288958978651%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="484" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Puffin_watching_(8958978651).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Approaching nesting birds too closely may jeopardise the colony.</div></div></div>
<p>Breeding colonies of Atlantic puffins provide an interesting spectacle for both bird watchers and tourists. For example, 4000 puffins nest each year on islands off the coast of Maine, and visitors can view them from tour boats that operate during the summers. The Project Puffin Visitor Center in <a href="/wiki/Rockland,_Maine" title="Rockland, Maine">Rockland</a> provides information on the birds and their lives, and on the other conservation projects being undertaken by the <a href="/wiki/National_Audubon_Society" title="National Audubon Society">National Audubon Society</a>, which runs the center.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Views of the colony on <a href="/wiki/Seal_Island_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Seal Island National Wildlife Refuge">Seal Island National Wildlife Refuge</a> can be viewed via live cams during the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
Similar tours operate in Iceland, the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland" class="mw-redirect" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting">Hunting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hunting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

.thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Fleygamenn_Trongisv%C3%A1gur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Puffin hunting"
