class="thumbcaption">Puffin hunters, <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, late 1890s</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Stora_dimun_puffins_for_kitchen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Puffin carcases" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Stora_dimun_puffins_for_kitchen.jpg/200px-Stora_dimun_puffins_for_kitchen.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Stora_dimun_puffins_for_kitchen.jpg/300px-Stora_dimun_puffins_for_kitchen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Stora_dimun_puffins_for_kitchen.jpg/400px-Stora_dimun_puffins_for_kitchen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">On <a href="/wiki/St%C3%B3ra_D%C3%ADmun" title="Stóra Dímun">Stóra Dímun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faroese_puffin" title="Faroese puffin">puffins</a> are still important food today</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Puffins have been hunted by man since time immemorial. Coastal communities and island dwellers with few natural resources at their disposal made good use of the seafood they found on their cliffs and shores. Puffins were caught and eaten fresh, salted in brine, or smoked and dried. Their feathers were used in bedding and their eggs were eaten, but not to the same extent as those of some other seabirds, being more difficult to extract from the nest. In most countries, Atlantic puffins are now protected by legislation, and in the countries where hunting is still permitted, strict laws prevent overexploitation. They are still caught and eaten in Iceland and the Faroe Islands,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> but calls have been made for an outright ban on hunting them in Iceland because of concern over the dwindling number of birds successfully raising chicks.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional means of capture varied across the birds' range and nets and rods were used in various ingenious ways. A typical device used in the Faroes was a <i>fleyg</i>. This was a long pole with a net on the end laid flat on the ground. A few dead puffins were strewn around to entice incoming birds to land, and the net was flicked upwards to scoop a bird from the air as it slowed before alighting. Most of the birds caught were subadults, and a skilled hunter could gather 200–300 in a day. Another method of capture, used in <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Scotland" title="St Kilda, Scotland">St Kilda</a>, involved the use of a flexible pole with a noose on the end. This was pushed along the ground towards the intended target, which advanced to inspect the noose as its curiosity overcame its caution. A flick of the wrist would flip the noose over the victim's head and it was promptly killed, before its struggles could alarm other birds nearby.<sup id="cite_ref-boag_18-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boag-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>112–113</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_puffin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_031_puffin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Stamp" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Faroe_stamp_031_puffin.jpg/170px-Faroe_stamp_031_puffin.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/Faroe_stamp_031_puffin.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="211" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_031_puffin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> 1978 postal stamp by Holger Philipsen</div></div></div>
<p>The Atlantic puffin is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_provincial_and_territorial_symbols" title="List of Canadian provincial and territorial symbols">official bird</a> symbol of the province of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a>, Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In August 2007, the Atlantic puffin was proposed as the official symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Canada" title="Liberal Party of Canada">Liberal Party of Canada</a> by its deputy leader <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ignatieff" title="Michael Ignatieff">Michael Ignatieff</a>, after he observed a colony of these birds and became fascinated by their behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The Norwegian municipality of <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A6r%C3%B8y" title="Værøy">Værøy</a> has an Atlantic puffin as its civic emblem.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Puffins are viewed with affection because they are colourful and full of character. They have been given a number of endearing names including "clowns of the sea" and "sea parrots", and juvenile puffins may be called "pufflings".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of islands have been named after the bird. The island of Lundy in the United Kingdom is reputed to derive its name from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Norse language">Norse</a> <i>lund-ey</i> or "puffin island".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> An alternative explanation has been suggested connected with another meaning of the word "lund" referring to a copse or wooded area. The <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> might have found the island a useful refuge and restocking point after their depredations on the mainland .<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The island issued its own <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coins</a>, and in 1929, its own <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">stamps</a> with denominations in "puffins".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Other countries and dependencies that have depicted Atlantic puffins on their stamps include <a href="/wiki/Alderney" title="Alderney">Alderney</a>, Canada, the Faroe Islands, France, <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">Guernsey</a>, Iceland, Ireland, the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Jersey</a>, Norway, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Pierre_et_Miquelon" class="mw-redirect" title="St Pierre et Miquelon">St Pierre et Miquelon</a>, and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The LPO, a French biodiversity charity focusing on the protection of birds, uses a pair of Atlantic puffins as its emblem.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The publisher of <a href="/wiki/Paperback" title="Paperback">paperbacks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penguin_Books" title="Penguin Books">Penguin Books</a>, introduced a range of books for children under the <a href="/wiki/Puffin_Books" title="Puffin Books">Puffin Books</a> brand in 1939. At first, these were nonfiction titles, but these were soon followed by a fiction list of well-known authors. The demand was so great that Puffin Book Clubs were introduced in schools to encourage reading, and a children's magazine called <i>Puffin Post</i> was established.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> A tradition exists on the Icelandic island of <a href="/wiki/Heimaey" title="Heimaey">Heimaey</a> for the children to rescue young puffins, a fact recorded in <a href="/wiki/Bruce_McMillan" title="Bruce McMillan">Bruce McMillan's</a> photo-illustrated children's book <i><a href="/wiki/Nights_of_the_Pufflings" title="Nights of the Pufflings">Nights of the Pufflings</a></i> (1995). The fledglings emerge from the nest and try to make their way to the sea, but sometimes get confused, perhaps by the street lighting, ending up by landing in the village. The children collect them and liberate them to the safety of the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-IcelandWeb_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IcelandWeb-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFGuthrieHowellKennedy1999" class="citation journal cs1">Guthrie, Daniel A.; Howell, Thomas, W.; Kennedy, George L.
